	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Immigration</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Movement of people into another country or region to which they are not native </div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a> or <a href="/wiki/Migration_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Migration (disambiguation)">Migration</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Immigrant" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Immigrant_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Immigrant (disambiguation)">Immigrant (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Immigration_by_country" title="Immigration by country">Immigration by country</a></div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Net_Migration_Rate.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Net_Migration_Rate.svg/400px-Net_Migration_Rate.svg.png" decoding="async" width="400" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Net_Migration_Rate.svg/600px-Net_Migration_Rate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Net_Migration_Rate.svg/800px-Net_Migration_Rate.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1035" data-file-height="531" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Net_Migration_Rate.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Net_migration_rate" title="Net migration rate">Net migration rates</a> for 2016: positive (blue), negative (orange), stable (green), and no data (gray)</div></div></div>

</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Legal_status" title="Template:Legal status"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Legal_status" title="Template talk:Legal status"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Legal_status&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Immigration</b> is the international movement of people into a destination <a href="/wiki/Country" title="Country">country</a> of which they are not natives or where they do not possess <a href="/wiki/Citizenship" title="Citizenship">citizenship</a> in order to settle or reside there, especially as <a href="/wiki/Permanent_residency" title="Permanent residency">permanent residents</a> or <a href="/wiki/Naturalization" title="Naturalization">naturalized</a> citizens, or to take up <a href="/wiki/Employment" title="Employment">employment</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Migrant_worker" title="Migrant worker">migrant worker</a> or temporarily as a <a href="/wiki/Foreign_worker" title="Foreign worker">foreign worker</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RCUK_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RCUK-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As for economic effects, research suggests that migration is beneficial both to the receiving and sending countries.<sup id="cite_ref-di_GiovanniLevchenko2015_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-di_GiovanniLevchenko2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WorldBank2016_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldBank2016-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Research, with few exceptions, finds that immigration on average has positive economic effects on the native population, but is mixed as to whether low-skilled immigration adversely affects low-skilled natives.<sup id="cite_ref-CardDustmann2012_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CardDustmann2012-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-IGM_Forum_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGM_Forum-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> Studies show that the elimination of barriers to migration would have profound effects on world <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">GDP</a>, with estimates of gains ranging between 67 and 147 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-Iregui2003_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iregui2003-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clemens2011_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clemens2011-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HamiltonWhalley1984_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HamiltonWhalley1984-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Development_economics" title="Development economics">Development economists</a> argue that reducing barriers to labor mobility between developing countries and developed countries would be one of the most efficient tools of poverty reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-Milanovic2015_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Milanovic2015-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mishra2014_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mishra2014-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:82_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:82-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The academic literature provides mixed findings for the relationship between <a href="/wiki/Immigration_and_crime" title="Immigration and crime">immigration and crime</a> worldwide, but finds for the <a href="/wiki/Immigration_and_crime_in_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Immigration and crime in the United States">United States</a> that immigration either has no impact on the crime rate or that it reduces the crime rate.<sup id="cite_ref-:532_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:532-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:362_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:362-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Research shows that country of origin matters for speed and depth of immigrant assimilation, but that there is considerable assimilation overall for both first- and second-generation immigrants.<sup id="cite_ref-VillarrealTamborini2018_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VillarrealTamborini2018-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Blau2015_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blau2015-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Research has found extensive evidence of <a href="/wiki/Discrimination_based_on_skin_color" title="Discrimination based on skin color">discrimination</a> against foreign born and minority populations in criminal justice, business, the economy, housing, health care, media, and politics in the United States and Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-ZschirntRuedin2016_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ZschirntRuedin2016-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Rich2014_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rich2014-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RehaviStarr2014_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RehaviStarr2014-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Enos2016_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Enos2016-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#Impact_on_the_sending_country"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">Impact on the sending country</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">17</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">18</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">19</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>
<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">20</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>
</ul>
</div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox mbox-small-left ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><a href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg" class="image"><img alt="[icon]" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" decoding="async" width="20" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="44" data-file-height="31" /></a></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. <small>You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Immigration&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.</small>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2015</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_human_migration" title="History of human migration">History of human migration</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Immigration_Schild.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Immigration_Schild.jpg/220px-Immigration_Schild.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Immigration_Schild.jpg/330px-Immigration_Schild.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Immigration_Schild.jpg/440px-Immigration_Schild.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2778" data-file-height="2087" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Immigration_Schild.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sign <i>Immigration</i> near the border between Mali and Mauritania; sponsored by EU</div></div></div>
<p>The term <i>immigration</i> was coined in the 17th century, referring to non-warlike population movements between the emerging <a href="/wiki/Nation_state" title="Nation state">nation states</a>.
</p><p>When people cross <a href="/wiki/Border" title="Border">national borders</a> during their migration, they are called migrants or immigrants (from Latin: <i>migrare</i>, wanderer) from the perspective of the country which they enter. From the perspective of the country which they leave, they are called emigrant or outmigrant.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">Sociology</a> designates immigration usually as <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">migration</a> (as well as <a href="/wiki/Emigration" title="Emigration">emigration</a> accordingly outward migration).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:UN_Stats.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/UN_Stats.jpg/250px-UN_Stats.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/UN_Stats.jpg/375px-UN_Stats.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/UN_Stats.jpg/500px-UN_Stats.jpg 2x" data-file-width="761" data-file-height="459" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:UN_Stats.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The global population of immigrants has grown since 1990 but has remained constant at around 3% of the world's population.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>As of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Immigration&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the number of international migrants has reached 244 million worldwide, which reflects a 41% increase since 2000. One third of the world's international migrants are living in just 20 countries. The largest number of international migrants live in the <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_States" title="Immigration to the United States">United States</a>, with 19% of the world's total. <a href="/wiki/Immigration_to_Germany" title="Immigration to Germany">Germany</a> and Russia host 12 million migrants each, taking the second and third place in countries with the most migrants worldwide. Saudi Arabia hosts 10 million migrants, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Modern_immigration_to_the_United_Kingdom" title="Modern immigration to the United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> (9 million) and the United Arab Emirates (8 million).<sup id="cite_ref-:15_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Between 2000 and 2015, Asia added more international migrants than any other major area in the world, gaining 26 million. Europe added the second largest with about 20 million. In most parts of the world, migration occurs between countries that are located within the same major area.<sup id="cite_ref-:15_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, the number of international migrants below the age of 20 reached 37 million, while 177 million are between the ages of 20 and 64. International migrants living in Africa were the youngest, with a median age of 29, followed by Asia (35 years), and Latin America/Caribbean (36 years), while migrants were older in Northern America (42 years), Europe (43 years), and Oceania (44 years).<sup id="cite_ref-:15_27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Migrants_in_the_world_2015-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Migrants_in_the_world_2015-en.svg/260px-Migrants_in_the_world_2015-en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="260" height="128" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Migrants_in_the_world_2015-en.svg/390px-Migrants_in_the_world_2015-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Migrants_in_the_world_2015-en.svg/520px-Migrants_in_the_world_2015-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1357" data-file-height="670" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Migrants_in_the_world_2015-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The number of migrants and migrant workers per country in 2015</div></div></div>
<p>Nearly half (43%) of all international migrants originate in Asia, and Europe was the birthplace of the second largest number of migrants (25%), followed by Latin America (15%). <a href="/wiki/Indian_diaspora" class="mw-redirect" title="Indian diaspora">India</a> has the largest diaspora in the world (16 million people), followed by <a href="/wiki/Mexican_diaspora" class="mw-redirect" title="Mexican diaspora">Mexico</a> (12 million) and <a href="/wiki/Russian_diaspora" title="Russian diaspora">Russia</a> (11 million).<sup id="cite_ref-:15_27-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:15-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2012_survey">2012 survey</span></h3>
<p>A 2012 survey by <a href="/wiki/The_Gallup_Organization" class="mw-redirect" title="The Gallup Organization">Gallup</a> found that given the opportunity, 640 million adults would migrate to another country, with 23% of these would-be immigrant choosing the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> as their desired future residence, while 7% of respondents, representing 45 million people, would choose the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>. The other top desired destination countries (those where an estimated 69 million or more adults would like to go) were <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Understanding_of_immigration">Understanding of immigration</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Multilingual_Pisnice_1858.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Multilingual_Pisnice_1858.JPG/220px-Multilingual_Pisnice_1858.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Multilingual_Pisnice_1858.JPG/330px-Multilingual_Pisnice_1858.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Multilingual_Pisnice_1858.JPG/440px-Multilingual_Pisnice_1858.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1968" data-file-height="1494" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Multilingual_Pisnice_1858.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The largest <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_people_in_the_Czech_Republic" title="Vietnamese people in the Czech Republic">Vietnamese</a> market in <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a>, also known as "Little Hanoi". In 2009, there were about 70,000 Vietnamese in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg/220px-Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="209" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg/330px-Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg/440px-Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="607" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ridley_road_market_dalston_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> has become multiethnic as a result of immigration.<sup id="cite_ref-FT1_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FT1-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>   In London in 2008, <a href="/wiki/Black_British" title="Black British">Black British</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Asian" title="British Asian">British Asian</a> children outnumbered white British children by about 3 to 2 in government-run schools.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>One theory of immigration distinguishes between push and pull factors.<sup id="cite_ref-knaw_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-knaw-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Push factors refer primarily to the motive for immigration from the country of origin. In the case of economic migration (usually labor migration), differentials in <a href="/wiki/Wage_rate" class="mw-redirect" title="Wage rate">wage rates</a> are common. If the value of wages in the new country surpasses the value of wages in one's native country, he or she may choose to migrate, as long as the costs are not too high. Particularly in the 19th century, economic expansion of the US increased immigrant flow, and nearly 15% of the population was <a href="/wiki/Foreign_born" title="Foreign born">foreign born</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> thus making up a significant amount of the labor force.
</p><p>As transportation technology improved, travel time, and costs decreased dramatically between the 18th and early 20th century. Travel across the Atlantic used to take up to 5 weeks in the 18th century, but around the time of the 20th century it took a mere 8 days.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Opportunity_cost" title="Opportunity cost">opportunity cost</a> is lower, the immigration rates tend to be higher.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Escape from <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a> (personal or for relatives staying behind) is a traditional push factor, and the availability of <a href="/wiki/Employment" title="Employment">jobs</a> is the related pull factor. <a href="/wiki/Natural_disasters" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural disasters">Natural disasters</a> can amplify poverty-driven migration flows. Research shows that for middle-income countries, higher temperatures increase emigration rates to urban areas and to other countries. For low-income countries, higher temperatures reduce emigration.<sup id="cite_ref-CattaneoPeri2016_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CattaneoPeri2016-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Emigration and immigration are sometimes mandatory in a contract of employment: religious <a href="/wiki/Missionary" title="Missionary">missionaries</a> and employees of <a href="/wiki/Transnational_corporations" class="mw-redirect" title="Transnational corporations">transnational corporations</a>, international <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organizations" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-governmental organizations">non-governmental organizations</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_service" title="Diplomatic service">diplomatic service</a> expect, by definition, to work "overseas". They are often referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Expatriates" class="mw-redirect" title="Expatriates">expatriates</a>", and their conditions of employment are typically equal to or better than those applying in the host country (for similar work).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Non-economic push factors include <a href="/wiki/Persecution" title="Persecution">persecution</a> (religious and otherwise), frequent abuse, <a href="/wiki/Bullying" title="Bullying">bullying</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oppression" title="Oppression">oppression</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_cleansing" title="Ethnic cleansing">ethnic cleansing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a>, risks to civilians during <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>, and social marginalization.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kislev2014_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kislev2014-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Political motives traditionally motivate refugee flows; for instance, people may emigrate in order to escape a <a href="/wiki/Dictatorship" title="Dictatorship">dictatorship</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Borjas2016_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borjas2016-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some migration is for personal reasons, based on a <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_relationship" title="Interpersonal relationship">relationship</a> (e.g. to be with family or a partner), such as in <a href="/wiki/Family_reunification" title="Family reunification">family reunification</a> or <a href="/wiki/Transnational_marriage" title="Transnational marriage">transnational marriage</a> (especially in the instance of a <a href="/wiki/Gender_imbalance" class="mw-redirect" title="Gender imbalance">gender imbalance</a>). Recent research has found gender, age, and cross-cultural differences in the ownership of the idea to immigrate.<sup id="cite_ref-Rubin2013_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rubin2013-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In a few cases, an individual may wish to immigrate to a new country in a form of transferred <a href="/wiki/Patriotism" title="Patriotism">patriotism</a>. Evasion of <a href="/wiki/Criminal_justice" title="Criminal justice">criminal justice</a> (e.g., avoiding <a href="/wiki/Arrest" title="Arrest">arrest</a>) is a personal motivation. This type of emigration and immigration is not normally legal, if a crime is internationally recognized, although criminals may disguise their identities or find other loopholes to evade detection. For example, there have been reports of <a href="/wiki/War_criminals" class="mw-redirect" title="War criminals">war criminals</a> disguising themselves as victims of war or conflict and then pursuing asylum in a different country.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Barriers to immigration come not only in legal form or political form; natural and social barriers to immigration can also be very powerful. Immigrants when leaving their country also leave everything familiar: their family, friends, support network, and culture. They also need to liquidate their assets, and they incur the expense of moving. When they arrive in a new country, this is often with many uncertainties including finding work,<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> where to live, new laws, new cultural norms, language or accent issues, possible <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>, and other exclusionary behavior towards them and their family.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Djajić2013_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Djajić2013-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_(Zaisa)_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG/260px-%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG" decoding="async" width="260" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG/390px-%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG/520px-%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_%28Zaisa%29_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2304" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%C4%8C%C3%AD%C5%BEov_(Zaisa)_-_preserved_part_of_Iron_curtain.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Iron_Curtain" title="Iron Curtain">Iron Curtain</a> in Europe was designed as a means of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Bloc_emigration_and_defection" title="Eastern Bloc emigration and defection">preventing emigration</a>. "It is one of the ironies of post-war European history that, once the freedom to travel for Europeans living under communist regimes, which had long been demanded by the West, was finally granted in 1989/90, travel was very soon afterwards made much more difficult by the West itself, and new barriers were erected to replace the Iron Curtain."  —Anita Böcker<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The politics of immigration have become increasingly associated with other issues, such as <a href="/wiki/National_security" title="National security">national security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terrorism</a>, especially in western Europe, with the presence of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> as a new major religion. Those with security concerns cite the <a href="/wiki/2005_French_riots" title="2005 French riots">2005 French riots</a> and point to the <a href="/wiki/Jyllands-Posten_Muhammad_cartoons_controversy" title="Jyllands-Posten Muhammad cartoons controversy">Jyllands-Posten Muhammad cartoons controversy</a> as examples of the value conflicts arising from immigration of <a href="/wiki/Muslims_in_Western_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslims in Western Europe">Muslims in Western Europe</a>. Because of all these associations, immigration has become an emotional political issue in many European nations.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies have suggested that some <a href="/wiki/Special_interest_group" class="mw-redirect" title="Special interest group">special interest groups</a> <a href="/wiki/Lobbying" title="Lobbying">lobby</a> for less immigration for their own group and more immigration for other groups since they see effects of immigration, such as increased labor competition, as detrimental when affecting their own group but beneficial when impacting other groups. A 2010 European study suggested that "employers are more likely to be pro-immigration than employees, provided that immigrants are thought to compete with employees who are already in the country. Or else, when immigrants are thought to compete with employers rather than employees, employers are more likely to be anti-immigration than employees."<sup id="cite_ref-Tamura2010_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tamura2010-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> A 2011 study examining the voting of US representatives on migration policy suggests that "representatives from more skilled labor abundant districts are more likely to support an open immigration policy towards the unskilled, whereas the opposite is true for representatives from more unskilled labor abundant districts."<sup id="cite_ref-FacchiniSteinhardt2011_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FacchiniSteinhardt2011-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Another contributing factor may be lobbying by earlier immigrants. The Chairman for the US Irish Lobby for Immigration Reform—which lobby for more permissive rules for immigrants, as well as special arrangements just for Irish people—has stated that "the Irish Lobby will push for any special arrangement it can get—'as will every other ethnic group in the country.<span style="padding-right:.15em;">'</span>"<sup id="cite_ref-irishlobbyusa_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-irishlobbyusa-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nclr_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nclr-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Immigrants are motivated to leave their former countries of citizenship, or habitual residence, for a variety of reasons, including a lack of local access to <a href="/wiki/Resources" class="mw-redirect" title="Resources">resources</a>, a desire for economic <a href="/wiki/Prosperity" title="Prosperity">prosperity</a>, to find or engage in paid work, to better their <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a>, <a href="/wiki/Family_reunification" title="Family reunification">family reunification</a>, <a href="/wiki/Retirement" title="Retirement">retirement</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_migrant" title="Environmental migrant">climate or environmentally induced</a> migration, <a href="/wiki/Exile" title="Exile">exile</a>, escape from prejudice, conflict or natural disaster, or simply the wish to change one's <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a>. <a href="/wiki/Commuting" title="Commuting">Commuters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">tourists</a> and other short-term stays in a destination country do not fall under the definition of immigration or migration, <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_industry" title="Seasonal industry">seasonal labour</a> immigration is sometimes included.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_migrant">Economic migrant</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Economic_migrant" title="Economic migrant">Economic migrant</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Indo-Bangladeshi_Barrier.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Indo-Bangladeshi_Barrier.JPG/280px-Indo-Bangladeshi_Barrier.JPG" decoding="async" width="280" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Indo-Bangladeshi_Barrier.JPG/420px-Indo-Bangladeshi_Barrier.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Indo-Bangladeshi_Barrier.JPG/560px-Indo-Bangladeshi_Barrier.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1066" data-file-height="582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Indo-Bangladeshi_Barrier.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh%E2%80%93India_border" title="Bangladesh–India border">Indo-Bangladeshi barrier</a> in 2007. <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> is building a <a href="/wiki/Separation_barrier" title="Separation barrier">separation barrier</a> along the 4,000 kilometer border with Bangladesh to prevent illegal immigration.</div></div></div>
<p>The term economic migrant refers to someone who has travelled from one region to another region for the purposes of seeking employment and an improvement in quality of life and access to resources. An economic migrant is distinct from someone who is a <a href="/wiki/Refugee" title="Refugee">refugee</a> fleeing persecution.
</p><p>Many countries have immigration and visa restrictions that prohibit a person entering the country for the purposes of gaining work without a valid work visa. As a violation of a <a href="/wiki/State_(polity)" title="State (polity)">State's</a> immigration laws a person who is declared to be an economic migrant can be refused entry into a country.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> estimates that <a href="/wiki/Remittance" title="Remittance">remittances</a> totaled $420 billion in 2009, of which $317 billion went to developing countries.<sup id="cite_ref-worldbank_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worldbank-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Laws_and_ethics">Laws and ethics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Africa-Xenophobia-001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/South_Africa-Xenophobia-001.jpg/220px-South_Africa-Xenophobia-001.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/South_Africa-Xenophobia-001.jpg/330px-South_Africa-Xenophobia-001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/South_Africa-Xenophobia-001.jpg/440px-South_Africa-Xenophobia-001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Africa-Xenophobia-001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>UNHCR tents at a refugee camp following episodes of <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia_in_South_Africa" title="Xenophobia in South Africa">anti-immigrant violence</a> in South Africa, 2008</div></div></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:204px;max-width:204px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:India_passport_entry_stamp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Entry stamp" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/India_passport_entry_stamp.jpg/200px-India_passport_entry_stamp.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7b/India_passport_entry_stamp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="240" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:India_passport_stamp.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Exit stamp" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/India_passport_stamp.jpg/200px-India_passport_stamp.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/India_passport_stamp.jpg/300px-India_passport_stamp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1f/India_passport_stamp.jpg 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="240" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Entry (top) and Exit (above) <a href="/wiki/Passport_stamp" title="Passport stamp">passport stamps</a> issued to a citizen of Germany by Indian immigration authorities at <a href="/wiki/Indira_Gandhi_International_Airport" title="Indira Gandhi International Airport">New Delhi airport</a>.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Treatment of migrants in host countries, both by governments, employers, and original population, is a topic of continual debate and criticism, and the violation of migrant human rights is an ongoing crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Protection_of_the_Rights_of_All_Migrant_Workers_and_Members_of_Their_Families" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations Convention on the Protection of the Rights of All Migrant Workers and Members of Their Families">United Nations Convention on the Protection of the Rights of All Migrant Workers and Members of Their Families</a>, has been ratified by 48 states, most of which are heavy exporters of cheap labor. Major migrant-receiving countries and regions – including Western Europe, North America, Pacific Asia, Australia, and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_states_(Middle_East)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gulf states (Middle East)">Gulf States</a> – have not ratified the Convention, even though they are host to the majority of international migrant workers.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_movement" title="Freedom of movement">freedom of movement</a> is often recognized as a <a href="/wiki/Civil_right" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil right">civil right</a> in many documents such as the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> (1948) and the <a href="/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights" title="International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights">International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights</a> (1966), the freedom only applies to movement within national borders and the ability to return to one's home state.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some proponents of immigration argue that the freedom of movement both within and between countries is a basic human right, and that the restrictive immigration policies, typical of nation-states, violate this human right of freedom of movement.<sup id="cite_ref-immigration_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-immigration-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Such arguments are common among ideologies like <a href="/wiki/Anarchism" title="Anarchism">anarchism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">libertarianism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-theanarchistlibrary_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-theanarchistlibrary-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>  As philosopher and <a href="/wiki/Open_border" title="Open border">Open borders</a> activist <a href="/wiki/Jacob_M._Appel" title="Jacob M. Appel">Jacob Appel</a> has written, "Treating human beings differently, simply because they were born on the opposite side of a national boundary, is hard to justify under any mainstream philosophical, religious or ethical theory."<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Where immigration is permitted, it is typically selective. As of 2003<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Immigration&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/Family_reunification" title="Family reunification">family reunification</a> accounted for approximately two-thirds of legal immigration to the US every year.<sup id="cite_ref-migrationinformation2_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-migrationinformation2-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Ethnic selection, such as the <a href="/wiki/White_Australia_policy" title="White Australia policy">White Australia policy</a>, has generally disappeared, but priority is usually given to the educated, skilled, and wealthy. Less privileged individuals, including the mass of poor people in low-income countries, cannot avail themselves of the legal and protected immigration opportunities offered by wealthy states. This inequality has also been criticized as conflicting with the principle of <a href="/wiki/Equal_opportunities" class="mw-redirect" title="Equal opportunities">equal opportunities</a>. The fact that the door is closed for the unskilled, while at the same time many developed countries have a huge demand for unskilled labor, is a major factor in <a href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration" title="Illegal immigration">illegal immigration</a>. The contradictory nature of this policy—which specifically disadvantages the unskilled immigrants while exploiting their labor—has also been criticized on ethical grounds.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Immigration policies which selectively grant freedom of movement to targeted individuals are intended to produce a net economic gain for the host country. They can also mean net loss for a poor donor country through the loss of the educated minority—a "<a href="/wiki/Brain_drain" class="mw-redirect" title="Brain drain">brain drain</a>". This can exacerbate the <a href="/wiki/Global_justice" title="Global justice">global inequality</a> in <a href="/wiki/Standards_of_living" class="mw-redirect" title="Standards of living">standards of living</a> that provided the motivation for the individual to migrate in the first place. One example of competition for skilled labour is active recruitment of <a href="/wiki/Health_worker" class="mw-redirect" title="Health worker">health workers</a> from developing countries by developed countries.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> There may however also be a "brain gain" to emigration, as migration opportunities lead to greater investments in education in developing countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Shrestha2017_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shrestha2017-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BeineDocquier2008_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BeineDocquier2008-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DinkelmanMariotti2016_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DinkelmanMariotti2016-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BatistaLacuesta2012_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BatistaLacuesta2012-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Overall, research suggests that migration is beneficial both to the receiving and sending countries.<sup id="cite_ref-di_GiovanniLevchenko2015_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-di_GiovanniLevchenko2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_effects">Economic effects</span></h2>
<p>A survey of leading economists shows a consensus behind the view that high-skilled immigration makes the average American better off.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> A survey of the same economists also shows support behind the notion that low-skilled immigration, while creating winners and losers, makes the average American better off.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> A survey of European economists shows a consensus that freer movement of people to live and work across borders within Europe makes the average European better off, and strong support behind the notion that it has not made low-skilled Europeans worse off.<sup id="cite_ref-IGM_Forum_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IGM_Forum-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/David_Card" title="David Card">David Card</a>, Christian Dustmann, and Ian Preston, "most existing studies of the economic impacts of immigration suggest these impacts are small, and on average benefit the native population".<sup id="cite_ref-CardDustmann2012_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CardDustmann2012-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> In a survey of the existing literature, Örn B Bodvarsson and Hendrik Van den Berg write, "a comparison of the evidence from all the studies... makes it clear that, with very few exceptions, there is no strong statistical support for the view held by many members of the public, mainly that immigration has an adverse effect on native-born workers in the destination country."<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overall_economic_prosperity">Overall economic prosperity</span></h3>
<p>Whereas the impact on the average native tends to be small and positive, studies show more mixed results for low-skilled natives, but whether the effects are positive or negative, they tend to be small either way.<sup id="cite_ref-Card2016_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Card2016-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FogedPeri2016_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FogedPeri2016-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Borjas2003_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Borjas2003-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ChassamboulliPeri2015_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChassamboulliPeri2015-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:0_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LonghiNijkamp2005_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LonghiNijkamp2005-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LonghiNijkamp2010_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LonghiNijkamp2010-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Okkerse2008_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Okkerse2008-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OttavianoPeri2012_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OttavianoPeri2012-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BattistiFelbermayr2014_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BattistiFelbermayr2014-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Card2005_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Card2005-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DustmannGlitz2009_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DustmannGlitz2009-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Constant2014_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Constant2014-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BlauMackie2017_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BlauMackie2017-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MitaritonnaOrefice2017_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MitaritonnaOrefice2017-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:22_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peri2018_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peri2018-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PeriYasenov2018_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PeriYasenov2018-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FT2_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FT2-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Bundling_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This claim has too many footnotes for reading to be smooth. (August 2018)">excessive citations</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Immigrants may often do types of work that natives are largely unwilling to do, contributing to greater economic prosperity for the economy as a whole: for instance, Mexican migrant workers taking up manual farm work in the United States has close to zero effect on native employment in that occupation, which means that the effect of Mexican workers on U.S. employment outside farm work was therefore most likely positive, since they raised overall economic productivity.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> Research indicates that immigrants are more likely to work in risky jobs than U.S.-born workers, partly due to differences in average characteristics, such as immigrants' lower English language ability and educational attainment.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2017 survey of the existing economic literature, studies on high-skilled migrants "rarely find adverse wage and employment consequences, and longer time horizons tend to show greater gains".<sup id="cite_ref-KerrKerr2017_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KerrKerr2017-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Competition from immigrants in a particular profession may aggravate <a href="/wiki/Underemployment" title="Underemployment">underemployment</a> in that profession,<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> but increase wages for other natives;<sup id="cite_ref-KerrKerr2017_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KerrKerr2017-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> for instance, a 2017 study in <i>Science</i> found that "the influx of foreign-born computer scientists since the early 1990s... increased the size of the US IT sector... benefited consumers via lower prices and more efficient products... raised overall worker incomes by 0.2 to 0.3% but decreased wages of U.S. computer scientists by 2.6 to 5.1%."<sup id="cite_ref-BoundKhanna2017_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BoundKhanna2017-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> A 2019 study found that foreign college workers in STEM occupations did not displace native college workers in STEM occupations, but instead had a positive impact on latters' wages.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> A 2019 study found that greater immigration led to less <a href="/wiki/Offshoring" title="Offshoring">off-shoring</a> by firms.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Research also suggests that diversity and immigration have a net positive effect on productivity <sup id="cite_ref-OttavianoPeri2006_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OttavianoPeri2006-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peri2012_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peri2012-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MitaritonnaOrefice2017_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MitaritonnaOrefice2017-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Razin2018_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Razin2018-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OttavianoPeri2018_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OttavianoPeri2018-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> and economic prosperity.<sup id="cite_ref-AlesinaHarnoss2016_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AlesinaHarnoss2016-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BoveElia2017_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BoveElia2017-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BoubtaneDumont2016_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BoubtaneDumont2016-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Immigration has also been associated with reductions in <a href="/wiki/Offshoring" title="Offshoring">offshoring</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-OttavianoPeri2018_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OttavianoPeri2018-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> A study by Harvard economist <a href="/wiki/Nathan_Nunn" title="Nathan Nunn">Nathan Nunn</a>, Yale economist Nancy Qian and LSE economist Sandra Sequeira found that the Age of Mass Migration (1850–1920) contributed to "higher incomes, higher productivity, more innovation, and more industrialization" in the short-run and "higher incomes, less poverty, less unemployment, higher rates of urbanization, and greater educational attainment" in the long-run for the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> Research also shows that migration to Latin America during the Age of Mass Migration had a positive impact on long-run economic development.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Studies show that the elimination of barriers to migration would have profound effects on world GDP, with estimates of gains ranging between 67–147.3% in the scenarios where billions of workers move from developing to developed countries.<sup id="cite_ref-Iregui2003_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Iregui2003-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clemens2011_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clemens2011-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HamiltonWhalley1984_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HamiltonWhalley1984-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Research also finds that migration leads to greater trade in goods and services,<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BrattiDeBenedictis2014_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BrattiDeBenedictis2014-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FoleyKerr2013_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FoleyKerr2013-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-DunlevyHutchinson2009_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DunlevyHutchinson2009-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ParsonsVézina2018_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ParsonsVézina2018-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> and increases in financial flows between the sending and receiving countries.<sup id="cite_ref-KuglerLevintal2018_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KuglerLevintal2018-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EggerErhardt2018_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EggerErhardt2018-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> Using 130 years of data on historical migrations to the United States, one study finds "that a doubling of the number of residents with ancestry from a given foreign country relative to the mean increases by 4.2 percentage points the probability that at least one local firm invests in that country, and increases by 31% the number of employees at domestic recipients of FDI from that country. The size of these effects increases with the ethnic diversity of the local population, the geographic distance to the origin country, and the ethno-linguistic fractionalization of the origin country."<sup id="cite_ref-BurchardiChaney2016_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BurchardiChaney2016-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> A 2017 study found that "immigrants' genetic diversity is significantly positively correlated with measures of U.S. counties' economic development [during the Age of Mass Migration]. There exists also a significant positive relationship between immigrants' genetic diversity in 1870 and contemporaneous measures of U.S. counties' average income."<sup id="cite_ref-AgerBrueckner2018_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AgerBrueckner2018-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some research suggests that immigration can offset some of the adverse effects of automation on native labor outcomes.<sup id="cite_ref-BassoPeri2017_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BassoPeri2017-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:13_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> By increasing overall demand, immigrants could push natives out of low-skilled manual labor into better paying occupations.<sup id="cite_ref-BassoPeri2017_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BassoPeri2017-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:13_128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> A 2018 study in the <i>American Economic Review</i> found that the <a href="/wiki/Bracero_program" title="Bracero program">Bracero program</a> (which allowed almost half a million Mexican workers to do seasonal farm labor in the United States) did not have any adverse impact on the labor market outcomes of American-born farm workers.<sup id="cite_ref-ClemensLewis2018_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClemensLewis2018-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2016 paper by University of Southern Denmark and University of Copenhagen economists found that the <a href="/wiki/Immigration_Act_of_1924" title="Immigration Act of 1924">1924 immigration restrictions</a> enacted in the United States impaired the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inequality">Inequality</span></h3>
<p>Overall immigration has not had much effect on native wage inequality<sup id="cite_ref-Card2009_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Card2009-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GreenGreen2016_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GreenGreen2016-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> but low-skill immigration has been linked to greater <a href="/wiki/Income_inequality" class="mw-redirect" title="Income inequality">income inequality</a> in the native population.<sup id="cite_ref-XuGarand2015_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-XuGarand2015-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GlenWeyl2018_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GlenWeyl2018-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> Greater openness to low-skilled immigration in wealthy countries would drastically reduce global income inequality.<sup id="cite_ref-GlenWeyl2018_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GlenWeyl2018-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiscal_effects">Fiscal effects</span></h3>
<p>A 2011 literature review of the economic impacts of immigration found that the net fiscal impact of migrants varies across studies but that the most credible analyses typically find small and positive fiscal effects on average.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> According to the authors, "the net social impact of an immigrant over his or her lifetime depends substantially and in predictable ways on the immigrant's age at arrival, education, reason for migration, and similar".<sup id="cite_ref-:0_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> According to a 2007 literature review by the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Budget_Office" title="Congressional Budget Office">Congressional Budget Office</a>, "Over the past two decades, most efforts to estimate the fiscal impact of immigration in the United States have concluded that, in aggregate and over the long term, tax revenues of all types generated by immigrants—both legal and unauthorized—exceed the cost of the services they use."<sup id="cite_ref-:18_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:18-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2018 study found that inflows of asylum seekers into Western Europe from 1985 to 2015 had a net positive fiscal impact.<sup id="cite_ref-AlbisBoubtane2018_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AlbisBoubtane2018-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maxmen2018_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maxmen2018-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Research has shown that EU immigrants made a net positive fiscal contribution to Denmark<sup id="cite_ref-MartinsenPonsRotger2017_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MartinsenPonsRotger2017-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> and the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FT2_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FT2-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> A 2017 study found that when Romanian and Bulgarian immigrants to the United Kingdom gained permission to acquire welfare benefits in 2014 that it had no discernible impact on the immigrants' use of welfare benefits.<sup id="cite_ref-RuhsWadsworth2017_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RuhsWadsworth2017-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> A paper by a group of French economists found that over the period 1980-2015, "international migration had a positive impact on the economic and fiscal performance of OECD countries."<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_refugees">Impact of refugees</span></h3>
<p>A 2017 survey of leading economists found that 34% of economists agreed with the statement "The influx of refugees into Germany beginning in the summer of 2015 will generate net economic benefits for German citizens over the succeeding decade", whereas 38% were uncertain and 6% disagreed.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> Studies of refugees' impact on native welfare are scant but the existing literature shows mixed results (negative, positive and no significant effects on native welfare).<sup id="cite_ref-FogedPeri2016_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FogedPeri2016-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TaylorFilipski2016_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TaylorFilipski2016-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Del_CarpioWagner2015_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Del_CarpioWagner2015-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MaystadtVerwimp2014_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MaystadtVerwimp2014-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BalkanTumen2016_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BalkanTumen2016-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-RefugeeImpact_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RefugeeImpact-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FakihIbrahim2015_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FakihIbrahim2015-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Tumen2016_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tumen2016-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ClemensHunt2017_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ClemensHunt2017-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:22_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:22-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Card2016_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Card2016-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EvansFitzgerald2017_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EvansFitzgerald2017-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Azarnert2018_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Azarnert2018-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Bundling_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This claim has too many footnotes for reading to be smooth. (August 2018)">excessive citations</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> According to economist Michael Clemens, "when economists have studied past influxes of refugees and migrants they have found the labor market effects, while varied, are very limited, and can in fact be positive."<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> A 2018 study in the <i>Economic Journal</i> found that Vietnamese refugees to the United States had a positive impact on American exports, as exports to Vietnam grew most in US states with larger Vietnamese populations.<sup id="cite_ref-ParsonsVézina2018_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ParsonsVézina2018-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> A 2018 study in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Science_Advances" title="Science Advances">Science Advances</a></i> found that asylum seekers entering Western Europe in the period 1985-2015 had a positive macroeconomic and fiscal impact.<sup id="cite_ref-AlbisBoubtane2018_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AlbisBoubtane2018-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Maxmen2018_139-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maxmen2018-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> A 2019 study found that the mass influx of 1.3 million Syrian refugees to Jordan (total population: 6.6 million) did not have harm the labor market outcomes of native Jordanians.<sup id="cite_ref-RefugeeImpact_153-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RefugeeImpact-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2017 paper by Evans and Fitzgerald found that refugees to the United States pay "$21,000 more in taxes than they receive in benefits over their first 20 years in the U.S."<sup id="cite_ref-EvansFitzgerald2017_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EvansFitzgerald2017-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> An internal study by the Department of Health and Human Services under the Trump administration, which was suppressed and not shown to the public, found that refugees to the United States brought in $63 billion more in government revenues than they cost the government.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/University_of_California,_Davis" title="University of California, Davis">University of California, Davis</a>, labor economist <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Peri" title="Giovanni Peri">Giovanni Peri</a>, the existing literature suggests that there are no economic reasons why the American labor market could not easily absorb 100,000 Syrian refugees in a year.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> A 2017 paper looking at the long-term impact of refugees on the American labor market over the period 1980–2010 found "that there is no adverse long-run impact of refugees on the U.S. labor market."<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Refugees integrate more slowly into host countries' labor markets than labor migrants, in part due to the loss and depreciation of human capital and credentials during the asylum procedure.<sup id="cite_ref-Bevelander2016_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bevelander2016-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Refugees tend to do worse in economic terms than natives, even when they have the same skills and language proficiencies of natives. For instance, a 2013 study of Germans in West-Germany who had been displaced from Eastern Europe during and after World War II showed that the forced German migrants did far worse economically than their native West-German counterparts decades later.<sup id="cite_ref-BauerBraun2013_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BauerBraun2013-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> Second-generation forced German migrants also did worse in economic terms than their native counterparts.<sup id="cite_ref-BauerBraun2013_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BauerBraun2013-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> A study of refugees to the United States found that "refugees that enter the U.S. before age 14 graduate high school and enter college at the same rate as natives. Refugees that enter as older teenagers have lower attainment with much of the difference attributable to language barriers and because many in this group are not accompanied by a parent to the U.S."<sup id="cite_ref-EvansFitzgerald2017_159-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EvansFitzgerald2017-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Refugees that entered the U.S. at ages 18–45, have "much lower levels of education and poorer language skills than natives and outcomes are initially poor with low employment, high welfare use and low earnings."<sup id="cite_ref-EvansFitzgerald2017_159-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EvansFitzgerald2017-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> But the authors of the study find that "outcomes improve considerably as refugees age."<sup id="cite_ref-EvansFitzgerald2017_159-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EvansFitzgerald2017-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2017 study found that the 0.5 million Portuguese who returned to Portugal from Mozambique and Angola in the mid-1970s lowered labor productivity and wages.<sup id="cite_ref-Mäkelä2017_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mäkelä2017-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> A 2018 paper found that the areas in Greece that took on a larger share of Greek Orthodox refugees from the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Turkish_War_(1919%E2%80%931922)" title="Greco-Turkish War (1919–1922)">Greco-Turkish War of 1919-1922</a> "have today higher earnings, higher levels of household wealth, greater educational attainment, as well as larger financial and manufacturing sectors."<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_of_undocumented_immigrants">Impact of undocumented immigrants</span></h3>
<p>Research on the economic effects of undocumented immigrants is scant but existing studies suggests that the effects are positive for the native population,<sup id="cite_ref-Palivos2008_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palivos2008-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Liu2010_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Liu2010-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> and public coffers.<sup id="cite_ref-:18_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:18-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:32_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:32-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> A 2015 study shows that "increasing deportation rates and tightening border control weakens low-skilled labor markets, increasing unemployment of native low-skilled workers. Legalization, instead, decreases the unemployment rate of low-skilled natives and increases income per native."<sup id="cite_ref-ChassamboulliPeri2015_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChassamboulliPeri2015-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Studies show that legalization of undocumented immigrants would boost the U.S. economy; a 2013 study found that granting legal status to undocumented immigrants would raise their incomes by a quarter (increasing U.S. GDP by approximately $1.4 trillion over a ten-year period),<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> and a 2016 study found that "legalization would increase the economic contribution of the unauthorized population by about 20%, to 3.6% of private-sector GDP."<sup id="cite_ref-EdwardsOrtega2016_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EdwardsOrtega2016-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> A 2018 National Bureau of Economic Research paper found that undocumented immigrants to the United States "generate higher surplus for US firms relative to natives, hence restricting their entry has a depressing effect on job creation and, in turn, on native labor markets."<sup id="cite_ref-ChassamboulliPeri2018_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChassamboulliPeri2018-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2017 study in the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Public_Economics" title="Journal of Public Economics">Journal of Public Economics</a></i> found that more intense immigration enforcement increased the likelihood that US-born children with undocumented immigrant parents would live in poverty.<sup id="cite_ref-Amuedo-Dorantes_Arenas-Arroyo2018_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amuedo-Dorantes_Arenas-Arroyo2018-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A paper by Spanish economists found that upon legalizing the undocumented immigrant population in Spain, the fiscal revenues increased by around €4,189 per newly legalized immigrant.<sup id="cite_ref-:32_171-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:32-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> The paper found that the wages of the newly legalized immigrants increased after legalization, some low-skilled natives had worse labor market outcomes and high-skilled natives had improved labor market outcomes.<sup id="cite_ref-:32_171-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:32-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2018 study found no evidence that apprehensions of undocumented immigrants in districts in the United States improved the labor market outcomes for American natives.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_on_the_sending_countries">Impact on the sending countries</span></h3>
<p>Research suggests that migration is beneficial both to the receiving and sending countries.<sup id="cite_ref-di_GiovanniLevchenko2015_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-di_GiovanniLevchenko2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WorldBank2016_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WorldBank2016-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> According to one study, welfare increases in both types of countries: "welfare impact of observed levels of migration is substantial, at about 5% to 10% for the main receiving countries and about 10% in countries with large incoming remittances".<sup id="cite_ref-di_GiovanniLevchenko2015_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-di_GiovanniLevchenko2015-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Branko_Milanovi%C4%87" title="Branko Milanović">Branko Milanović</a>, country of residency is by far the most important determinant of global income inequality, which suggests that the reduction in labor barriers would significantly reduce global income inequality.<sup id="cite_ref-Milanovic2015_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Milanovic2015-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FT3_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FT3-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> A study of equivalent workers in the United States and 42 developing countries found that "median wage gap for a male, unskilled (9 years of schooling), 35 year-old, urban formal sector worker born and educated in a developing country is P$15,400 per year at purchasing power parity".<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> A 2014 survey of the existing literature on emigration finds that a 10 percent emigrant supply shock would increase wages in the sending country by 2–5.5%.<sup id="cite_ref-Mishra2014_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mishra2014-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact_on_global_poverty">Impact on global poverty</span></h3>
